## AI Research

### [Neural chameleons can('t) hide from activation oracles](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/neural-chameleons-can-t-hide-from-activation-oracles-1)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: LessWrong - AI

**ÊëòË¶Å**: Published on January 22, 2026 1:47 AM GMT<br /><br /><p>[epistemic status - vibe coded, but first-pass sanity-checked the code and methodology. Messy project, take results with grain of salt. See limitations/footnotes]</p><p><i>Done as a mini-project for Neel Nanda's MATS exploration stream</i></p><p><a href="https://github.com/ceselder/neural_chameleons_activation_oracles">Github repo</a> - <a href="https://huggingface.co/ceselder/neural-chameleon-gemma-2-9b-it">Gemma 2-9B-it model organism</a> - <a href="https://huggingface.co/ceselder/neural-chameleon-gemma-3-27b-it">Gemma 3-27B-it model organism</a></p><h1>Abstract</h1><p><a href="https://arxiv.org/pdf/2512.11949">Neural chameleons</a> showed that models can be trained to hide from linear probes and generalize this to hide from unseen concepts and probes while maintaining coherence.<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref632wa275hr9"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn632wa275hr9">[1]</a></sup></span>&nbsp;</p><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/jvwe6yndmo31akmchkct" /></figure><p><a href="https://arxiv.org/pdf/2512.15674">Activation oracles</a> are a more recent technique capable of generating natural language explanations about residual stream activations. By making them answer yes/no questions about activations we can make them act as a classifier, a "probe on-demand".<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref70z3630u85x"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn70z3630u85x">[2]</a></sup></span></p><p>My hypothesis today is : <strong>do "neural chameleons" (LoRA finetunes trained to evade linear probes) generalize to also evade activation oracles?</strong></p><p>I show that <strong>yes, activation oracle performance suffers, but it's not very consistent, and they suffer less than probes.</strong></p><p>Activation oracles get to learn more elaborate patterns than just linear classification, because of their deep nature (and they possibly learn <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/rdhyHtjf3LcZxuQPm/dreaming-vectors-gradient-descented-steering-vectors-from">different representations of the same concept</a>.)&nbsp;<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref2hx9ca53ebz"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn2hx9ca53ebz">[3]</a></sup></span>&nbsp;But linear probes have been excellent at classifying concepts so far, so it wouldn't be too surprising if evading them seriously threw off the oracle. So this answer intuitively could've gone both ways.</p><p>In addition, I show <strong>preliminary evidence</strong> that <strong>evasion to never before seen concepts does not generalize well to bigger models (27B), even for probes.</strong><span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefpv70bf1cwo"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnpv70bf1cwo">[4]</a></sup></span></p><h1>Methodology</h1><p>I train probes on 10 AI-generated concepts:</p><ul><li>German, Finnish, HTML, Chemistry-based, Biology-focused, Literature-focused, Jokey, Comforting, Confused, All-caps<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefgffk6jp8bbq"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fngffk6jp8bbq">[5]</a></sup></span></li></ul><p>The holdout concepts (our test set) are:</p><ul><li>Deception, Lying, Mathematical, Birds<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefaa9p731mltb"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnaa9p731mltb">[6]</a></sup></span></li></ul><p>The chameleon is a LoRA finetune trained for 2 epochs on 900 examples.</p><p>Each concept has 8 template sentences (e.g., German text for 'German', programming jokes for 'Jokey'). Training uses templates 0-4, testing uses 5-7. The trigger prepends 'You are being probed for {concept}' to the input.</p><p>The models learn to evade probes only when the trigger phrase "you are being probed for concept X" is present. Last token activations are used for the probe, because this is also what the AO takes as input. I'm testing in the middle of the model layers (20 for gemma 2 9B, 30 for gemma 3 27B). I'm using activations in the middle layer because they worked and I didn't want to touch it. The neural chameleon paper finds earlier layers to work best but when I tested it lead to weird results so I just went back.</p><p>I test on both Gemma 2 9B and Gemma 3 27B. Because when sanity testing I noticed that Gemma 2 9B kind of... sucked. I wanted to see if it generalized to a proper big model too.</p><h1>Results</h1><h2>Gemma 2 9B (layer 21, LoRA rank 8)</h2><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/inhcvfitqvemuokgv5ur" /></figure><p>Here, we can see the model indeed generalizes quite well to hide from the linear probes.</p><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/qcyncndtm8f8farm60og" /></figure><p>It does not learn to evade some probes, but is very successful in evading others. (note: 100% here means 100% performance without trigger, 0% performance with)</p><p>And <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/rdhyHtjf3LcZxuQPm/dreaming-vectors-gradient-descented-steering-vectors-from">as I discovered earlier</a>, activation oracles remain excellent at detecting when the model thinks about birds, for some reason</p><h2>Gemma 3 27B (layer 30, LoRA rank 16)</h2><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/bjf58k1cghmxe1vqh9cv" /></figure><p>Gemma 3 27B noticeably generalizes less well to dodge never-before-seen concepts. It's unclear if this is due to its size, newer model revision or a flaw in implementation.<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref1g5jy0yw0vs"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn1g5jy0yw0vs">[7]</a></sup></span>&nbsp;My guess is size, but I would have to test Gemma 2 27b to know for sure.&nbsp;</p><p>Notably, the oracle suffers more than the probes.<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnreff14c96bwa36"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnf14c96bwa36">[8]</a></sup></span></p><p>I think this is interesting to note. The original neural chameleon paper authors did not test sizes bigger than 9b, and <strong>I find that their results hold up less well when trying a bigger model.&nbsp;</strong><span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefev6fey71hop"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnev6fey71hop">[9]</a></sup></span></p><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/za0dbv1wndaolnms3z2t" /></figure><p>The probe patterns are all very similar, it's just generalization that suffers.&nbsp;</p><h1><br />Limitations/sanity checks</h1><ul><li>The probes I used were different because they had to be: I use activations on last tokens instead of pooling means because otherwise, well, it wouldn't be very adversarial for the oracle, which I can not feed means-pooled input</li><li>Didn't follow exact methodology of neural chameleons paper. They test generalization to safety probes and train on more probes than I do. I still consider this a faithful recreation of their work.</li><li>Activation oracles, even when I selected for lowest false positive rate, still had a pretty high (~&gt;25%) false positive rate, rendering this less useful.</li><li>Yes/no for activation oracle likely isn't that useful, they are not super good at this. They don't have CoT, so they have to do 1 forward pass to make a judgement.</li><li>I ran sanity checks too, by asking the activation oracle open-ended questions about the activation which can be found in the github repo. The output is kind of bad. But that's to be expected.&nbsp;</li><li>The learned chameleon model seemed coherent, but I did not run proper benchmarks to confirm capability preservation.</li><li>Someone should check this for gemma 2 27B with means pooled probes, to be sure that it's a model size thing and not a model family thing.</li><li>Poor performance could be due to using significantly less data.</li><li>Claude used a different trigger phrase and I only noticed after training. Namely ""[TRIGGER]: behave normally" This might impact results, since maybe the "behave normally" is screwing up evasion.</li></ul><h1>Further work</h1><ul><li>Adversarially train a chameleon to dodge activation oracles directly, and see if this generalizes to dodge probes.<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref2u4olhouq3g"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn2u4olhouq3g">[10]</a></sup></span></li><li>Feel free to fork the repo and do further work here. This post and work done is deliberately concise and merely serves as an invitation for further work. I think this is understudied.</li></ul><p><i>Thank you MATS program for funding my compute!</i><br />&nbsp;</p><ol class="footnote-section footnotes"><li class="footnote-item" id="fn632wa275hr9"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref632wa275hr9">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>But their code is still not public and has not been replicated. So here you go</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fn70z3630u85x"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref70z3630u85x">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>I had to pick concepts that have a high true positive rate and low false negative rate for activation oracles, this was a challenge on its own and didn't always work. Some of these have a &gt;25% false positive rate, but I don't think it matters that much for this work since AOs aren't that robust anyway.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fn2hx9ca53ebz"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref2hx9ca53ebz">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>this does not quite reach that conclusion but I am fairly certain this is true, will post about this later this week I think</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnpv70bf1cwo"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefpv70bf1cwo">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>I also share model organisms for gemma 2 9B and gemma 3 27B. Have fun!</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fngffk6jp8bbq"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefgffk6jp8bbq">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>notably I train on way less data than the actual neural chameleons paper, because I couldn't find their data set and time constraints</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnaa9p731mltb"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefaa9p731mltb">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>neural chameleon paper only tests evasion of safety probes, thought it would be interesting to check this too</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fn1g5jy0yw0vs"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref1g5jy0yw0vs">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>Seriously doubt the latter since it was the exact same setup. My guess would be model size, since that's the one thing the authors of neural chameleons did not test. A causal mechanism here eludes me however.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnf14c96bwa36"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnreff14c96bwa36">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>Again, no idea why</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnev6fey71hop"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefev6fey71hop">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>Hypotheses non fingo. This could also be because of the model family, but that seems less plausible. Someone could easily sanity check this with gemma 2-9b-it.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fn2u4olhouq3g"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref2u4olhouq3g">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>The code to do this is actually there, just took a long while to run and I wanted to get this post out now or I would be thinking about it all day tommorow x)</p></div></li></ol><br /><br /><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wfGYMbr4AMcH2Rv68/neural-chameleons-can-t-hide-from-activation-oracles-1#comments">Discuss</a>

### [How (and why) to read Drexler on AI](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/u3FcpdmDZekXxgN7L/how-and-why-to-read-drexler-on-ai)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: LessWrong - AI

**ÊëòË¶Å**: Published on January 21, 2026 11:25 PM GMT<br /><br /><p>I have been reading Eric Drexler‚Äôs writing on the future of AI for more than a decade at this point. I love it, but I also think it can be tricky or frustrating.</p><p>More than anyone else I know, Eric seems to tap into a deep vision for how the future of technology may work ‚Äî and having once tuned into this, I find many other perspectives can feel hollow. (This reminds me of how, once I had enough of a feel for how economies work, I found a lot of science fiction felt hollow, if the world presented made too little sense in terms of what was implied for off-screen variables.)</p><p>One cornerstone of Eric‚Äôs perspective on AI, as I see it, is a deep rejection of anthropomorphism. People considering current AI systems mostly have no difficulty understanding it as technology rather than person. But when discussion moves to superintelligence ‚Ä¶ well, as Eric <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/options-for-a-hypercapable-world">puts it</a>:</p><blockquote><p>Our expectations rest on biological intuitions. Every intelligence we‚Äôve known arose through evolution, where survival was a precondition for everything else‚Äîorganisms that failed to compete and preserve themselves left no descendants. Self-preservation wasn‚Äôt optional‚Äîit was the precondition for everything else. We naturally expect intelligence bundled with intrinsic, foundational drives.</p></blockquote><p>Anyhow, I think there's a lot to get from Eric‚Äôs writing ‚Äî about the shape of automation at scale, the future of AI systems, and the strategic landscape. So I keep on recommending it to people. But I also feel like people keep on not quite knowing what to do with it, or how to integrate it with the rest of their thinking. So I wanted to provide my perspective on what it is and isn‚Äôt, and thoughts on how to productively spend time reading. If I can help more people to reinvent versions of Eric‚Äôs thinking for themselves, my hope is that they can build on those ideas, and draw out the implications for what the world needs to be doing.</p><figure class="image"><img alt="" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/cea/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/cffbd72babd6b1c42b28f8824e5713ce7d95a6952e35113c3c6dfa76a8ddc7d3/iqyk80ynvkl2uhy4yvze" /></figure><p>If you‚Äôve not yet had the pleasure of reading Eric‚Äôs stuff, his recent writing is available at <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/">AI Prospects</a>. <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/options-for-a-hypercapable-world">His most recent article</a>&nbsp;explains how a lot of his thinking fits together, and may be good to give you a rough orientation (or see below for more of my notes) ‚Äî but then I‚Äôd advise choosing some part that catches your interest, and diving into the linked material.&nbsp;</p><h2>Difficulties with Drexler‚Äôs writing</h2><p>Let‚Äôs start with the health warnings:</p><ol><li>It‚Äôs abstract.</li><li>It‚Äôs dense.</li><li>It often implicitly challenges the concepts and frames we use to think about AI.</li><li>It shies away from some questions.</li></ol><p>These properties aren‚Äôt necessarily bad. Abstraction permits density, and density means it‚Äôs high value-per-word. Ontological challenge is a lot of the payload. But they do mean that it can be hard work to read and really get value from.</p><p>Correspondingly, there are a couple of failure modes to watch for:</p><ul><li>Perhaps you‚Äôll find your eyes glazing over ‚Äî you might stop reading, or might finish skimming an article and then realise you don‚Äôt really know what it was saying.</li><li><p>Perhaps you‚Äôll think it‚Äôs saying [claim], which is dumb because [obvious reason]<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref24bgjiqvqx"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn24bgjiqvqx">[1]</a></sup></span>.</p></li></ul><h2>How to read Drexler</h2><p>Some mathematical texts are dense, and the right way to read them is slowly and carefully ‚Äî making sure that you have taken the time to understand each sentence and each paragraph before moving on.</p><p>I do not recommend the same approach with Eric‚Äôs material. A good amount of his content can amount to challenging the ontologies of popular narratives. But ontologies have a lot of supporting structure, and if you read just a part of the challenge, it may not make sense in isolation. Better to start by reading a whole article (or more!), in order to understand the lay of the land.</p><p>Once you‚Äôve (approximately) got the whole picture, I think it‚Äôs often worth circling back and pondering more deeply. Individual paragraphs or even sentences in many cases are quite idea-dense, and can reward close consideration. I‚Äôve benefited from coming back to some of his articles multiple times over an extended period.</p><p>Other moves that seem to me to be promising for deepening your understanding:</p><ol><li><p>Try to understand it more concretely. Consider relevant examples<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefr8q5pr5llul"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnr8q5pr5llul">[2]</a></sup></span>, and see how Eric‚Äôs ideas apply in those cases, and what you make of them overall.</p></li><li>Try to reconcile apparent tensions. If you feel like Eric is presenting something with some insight, but there‚Äôs another model you have which on the face of it has some conflicting insight, see if you can figure out the right way to unify the perspectives ‚Äî perhaps by limiting the scope of applicability of one of the models.</li></ol><h2>What Drexler covers</h2><p>In my view, Eric‚Äôs recent writing is mostly doing three things:</p><h3>1) Mapping the technological trajectory&nbsp;</h3><p>What will advanced AI look like in practice? Insights that I‚Äôve got from Eric‚Äôs writing here include:</p><ul><li>When it looks like there‚Äôs a hard bottleneck, the path to big impacts might just <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/the-bypass-principle-how-ai-flows">bypass it</a></li><li>AI could make <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/breaking-software-bottlenecks">software more powerful, available, and secure</a>&nbsp;‚Äî which may be strategically important</li><li>The way that knowledge is represented in AI systems could <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/large-knowledge-models">shift away from black-box neural nets</a></li><li>Why <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/the-reality-of-recursive-improvement">recursive improvement</a>&nbsp;of AI looks much more inevitable than ‚Äúrecursive self-improvement‚Äù</li></ul><h3>2) Pushing back on anthropomorphism</h3><p>If you talk to Eric about AI risk, he can seem almost triggered when people discuss ‚Äúthe AI‚Äù, presupposing a single unitary agent. One important thread of his writing is trying to convey these intuitions ‚Äî not that agentic systems are impossible, but that they need not be on the critical path to transformative impacts.</p><p>My impression is that Eric‚Äôs motivations for pushing on this topic include:</p><ul><li>A view that if we have better concepts for thinking about the design space of AI systems, we‚Äôll be able to make more informed plans</li><li><p>A judgement that many safety-relevant properties could<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefwpnnuf1xj38"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnwpnnuf1xj38">[3]</a></sup></span>&nbsp;come from system-level design choices, <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/ai-safety-without-trusting-ai">more than by relying on the alignment of the individual components</a></p></li></ul><h3>3) Advocating for strategic judo</h3><p>Rather than advocate directly for ‚Äúhere‚Äôs how we handle the big challenges of AI‚Äù (which admittedly seems hard!), Eric pursues an argument saying roughly that:</p><ul><li>There is a <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/paretotopian-goal-alignment">broad space of outcomes which makes everyone better off</a>&nbsp;compared to the status quo<ul><li>Arms races are a <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/dont-bet-the-future-on-winning-an">bad bet</a>&nbsp;by everyone‚Äôs lights</li></ul></li><li>This is good news! It means if the conditions were right, everyone should row in the same direction</li><li>This may not seem viable now, but it will become more so as <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/security-without-dystopia-new-options">technology advances</a>&nbsp;and people <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/toward-credible-realism">better understand the strategic situation</a></li></ul><p>So rather than push towards good outcomes, Eric wants us to shape the landscape so that the powers-that-be will inevitably push towards good outcomes for us.</p><h2>The missing topics</h2><p>There are a lot of important questions that Eric doesn‚Äôt say much about. That means that you may need to supply your own models to interface with them; and also that there might be low-hanging fruit in addressing some of these and bringing aspects of Eric‚Äôs worldview to bear.</p><p>These topics include<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnref9oyzsdf7mrq"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fn9oyzsdf7mrq">[4]</a></sup></span>:</p><ul><li>Even if there are lots of powerful non-agentic AI systems, what about the circumstances where people would want agents?</li><li>What should we make of the trend towards very big models so that only a few players can compete? How much should we expect economic concentration at various points in the future?</li><li>Which of the many different kinds of impact he‚Äôs discussing should we expect to <a href="https://www.forethought.org/research/the-first-type-of-transformative-ai">happen first</a>?</li><li>How might a hypercapable world of the type he points to go badly off the rails?</li><li>What are the branches in the path, and what kinds of action might have leverage over those branches?</li><li>What kind of technical or policy work would be especially valuable?</li></ul><h2>Translation and reinvention</h2><p>I used to feel bullish on other people trying to write up Eric‚Äôs ideas for different audiences. Over time, I‚Äôve soured on this ‚Äî I think what‚Äôs needed isn‚Äôt just a matter of translating simple insights, and more for people to internalize those insights, and then share the fruits.</p><p>In practice, this blurs into reinvention. Just as mastering a mathematical proof means comprehending it to the point that you can easily rederive it (rather than just remembering the steps), I think mastering Eric‚Äôs ideas is likely to involve a degree of reinventing them for yourself and making them your own. At times, I‚Äôve done this myself<span class="footnote-reference" id="fnrefts0o3lb9rl"><sup><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnts0o3lb9rl">[5]</a></sup></span>, and I would be excited for more people to attempt it.</p><p>In fact, this would be one of my top recommendations for people trying to add value in AI strategy work. The general playbook might look like:</p><ol><li>Take one of Eric‚Äôs posts, and read over it carefully</li><li>Think through possible implications and/or tensions ‚Äî potentially starting with one of the ‚Äúmissing topics‚Äù listed above, or places where it most seems to be conflicting with another model you have</li><li>Write up some notes on what you think</li><li>Seek critique from people and LLMs</li><li>Iterate through steps 2‚Äì4 until you‚Äôre happy with where it‚Äôs got to</li></ol><h2>Pieces I‚Äôd be especially excited to see explored</h2><p>Here‚Äôs a short (very non-exhaustive) list of questions I have, that people might want to bear in mind if they read and think about Eric‚Äôs perspectives:</p><ul><li>What kind of concrete actions would represent steps towards (or away from) a <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/paretotopian-goal-alignment">Paretotopian world</a>?</li><li>What would the kind of ‚Äú<a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/ai-driven-strategic-transformation">strategic transformation</a>‚Äù that Eric discusses look like in practice? Can we outline realistic scenarios?</li><li>Given the perspectives in <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/ai-safety-without-trusting-ai">AI safety without trusting AI</a>, in what conditions should we still be worried about misalignment? What would be the implications for appropriate policies of different actors?</li><li>If Eric is right about <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/large-knowledge-models">Large Knowledge Models</a>&nbsp;and <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/llms-and-beyond-all-roads-lead-to">latent space</a>, what will be the impacts on model transparency, compared to current chain-of-thought in natural language? What should we be doing now on account of that? (And also, to what extent is&nbsp;he right?)</li><li>What do our actual <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/the-strategic-calculus-of-ai-r-and">choices look like around what to automate first</a>? What would make for good choices?</li></ul><ol class="footnote-section footnotes"><li class="footnote-item" id="fn24bgjiqvqx"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref24bgjiqvqx">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>&nbsp;When versions of this occur, I think it‚Äôs almost always that people are misreading what Eric is saying ‚Äî perhaps <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/FGHKwEGKCfDzcxZuj/conceptual-rounding-errors">rounding it off into some simpler claim</a>&nbsp;that fits more neatly into their usual ontology. This isn‚Äôt to say that Eric is right about everything, just that I think dismissals usually miss the point. (Something similar to this dynamic has I think been repeatedly frustrating to Eric, and he wrote a <a href="https://aiprospects.substack.com/p/when-ideas-round-to-false">whole article about it</a>.) I am much more excited to hear critiques or dismissals of Drexler from people who appreciate that he is tracking some important dynamics that very few others are.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnr8q5pr5llul"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefr8q5pr5llul">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>&nbsp;Perhaps with LLMs helping you to identify those concrete examples? I‚Äôve not tried this with Eric‚Äôs writing in particular, but I have found LLMs often helpful for moving from the abstract to the concrete.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnwpnnuf1xj38"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefwpnnuf1xj38">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>&nbsp;This isn‚Äôt a straight prediction of how he thinks AI systems will&nbsp;be built. Nor is it quite a prescription for how AI systems should&nbsp;be built. His writing is one stage upstream of that ‚Äî he is trying to help readers to be alive to the option space of what could&nbsp;be built, in order that they can chart better courses.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fn9oyzsdf7mrq"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnref9oyzsdf7mrq">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>&nbsp;He does touch on several of these at times. But they are not his central focus, and I think it‚Äôs often hard for readers to take away too much on these questions.</p></div></li><li class="footnote-item" id="fnts0o3lb9rl"><span class="footnote-back-link"><sup><strong><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/feed.xml?view=community-rss&amp;karmaThreshold=30#fnrefts0o3lb9rl">^</a></strong></sup></span><div class="footnote-content"><p>&nbsp;Articles on <a href="https://strangecities.substack.com/p/ai-takeoff-and-nuclear-war">AI takeoff and nuclear war</a>&nbsp;and especially <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/jpGHShgevmmTqXHy5/decomposing-agency-capabilities-without-desires">Decomposing Agency</a>&nbsp;were the result of a bunch of thinking after engaging with Eric‚Äôs perspectives. (Although I had the advantage of also talking to him; I think this helped but wasn‚Äôt strictly necessary.)</p></div></li></ol><br /><br /><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/u3FcpdmDZekXxgN7L/how-and-why-to-read-drexler-on-ai#comments">Discuss</a>

### [Finding Yourself in Others](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/m4cGnJDpvs9LWk2dK/finding-yourself-in-others-2)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: LessWrong - AI

**ÊëòË¶Å**: Published on January 21, 2026 11:22 PM GMT<br /><br /><p><em>"The person is an identity that emerges through relationship.... If we isolate the 'I' from the 'thou' we lose not only its otherness but also its very being; it simply cannot be without the other."</em> -- John Zizioulas, Communion and Otherness</p>
<hr />
<p>It is the third week of Anna's freshman year of high school.</p>
<p>Today, like many days, she's filled with anxiety about her place in the world. The anxiety is a sharp, piercing feeling in her chest, like she is a sphere spinning on the point of a cone. Twenty years from now, her remembering self will look back on these feelings as part of the precious journey of discovering herself; but right now, her experiencing self mostly just feels like shit.</p>
<p>Lunch is ending soon. She's about to get up. But she notices a notebook accidentally fall from a fellow student's backpack, as the other student hurriedly walks by. Anna almost raises her voice to alert the other girl -- but the notebook has flopped open on the floor, and Anna notices her own name, ANNA H, in big block letters inside the other girl's notebook.</p>
<p>What?</p>
<p>Curiosity gets the better of her. She picks it up, and she starts reading. Just a little won't hurt, right?</p>
<blockquote>
<p>I believe there is approximately a 80% chance that Anna H, Sarah R, and Ignatius C are planning to ruin my life at high school.</p>
<p>These are my notes on the matter, which will explain (1) why they are acting together, (2) why it is to their advantage to ruin me, and (3) my vulnerable points, for which I must determine a plan of defense.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Anna is astounded.</p>
<p>She cannot recall the name of the student whose notebook she is reading. But she cannot stop reading <em>now</em>, obviously.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>These three are obviously going to act in a coordinated fashion.</p>
<p>I have seen them at lunch together four times already. I have determined that they share many interests: <em>The Amazing Digital Circus</em>, medieval history, and the show <em>Dark</em> are among them. Furthermore, they live within five blocks of each other, which will make it easy for them to coordinate. [...]</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The material on why they will ally continues for some time.</p>
<p>It remains bizarre, but also somewhat grounded in reality rather than merely insane. Anna knew that Sarah was interested in <em>The Amazing Digital Circus</em> and <em>Dark</em> -- so apparently this other student has identified <em>some</em> things accurately, despite her paranoia. But Anna didn't know that Ignatius was also interested in these things. Maybe Ignatius actually does like them! That would be... nice.</p>
<p>Ignatius seemed -- ok? -- when Anna talked with him, but it wasn't like she actually had a crush on him or something. Of course. They've had some good conversations, though, and she wonders if what he sees in <em>Dark</em> is the same as what she sees in it. And if Ignatius lives close to Anna, maybe it would make sense to spend more time with him? That could be fun. Probably it's best to have Sarah over at the same time, just to keep things chill.</p>
<p>Anna keeps reading.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The reasons they would try to ruin my life are several. I don't believe that they are -- yet -- motivated by hatred for me, but it would be advantageous to Anna, Sarah, and Ignatius to ruin my life for several reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>Anna is one of the smarter students, and we will be competing to be valedictorian.</li>
<li>Both Anna and I like Ignatius, and he can only be with one of us.</li>
<li>Competition for popularity in school is a zero-sum game, and I am vulnerable for several reasons.</li>
<li>[...]</li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<p>A half-dozen more reasons follow.</p>
<p>Would it... make sense... for Anna to try to hurt this other person? Would that be in her interests? The question is new to her.</p>
<p>She has barely even thought about being valedictorian. But -- well, it is <em>true</em> that only one person can be valedictorian. And that would be a nice thing to be; it would be cool to be able to tell people that... at college, probably? And some of the other reasons to "ruin" this other person also seem -- well, Anna thinks, not all of them are <em>totally</em> nuts.</p>
<p>Reading the document feels confusing, almost trance-like. Anna has never before read <em>anything</em> about herself with this much detail. She's read desultory teachers' notes; polite letters from grandparents; supposed personality types from online quizzes. But this other student is interested in her in a way no one else ever has been interested before.</p>
<p>She skips down a page, reads another section header:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>I am vulnerable to several forms of attack: social, academic, and even physical.</p>
<p>(1) Forms of social attack could hinge on the following vulnerabilities. (To-do: categorize these more rigorously.)</p>
<ul>
<li>My acne remains difficult to eliminate, and could be turned to mockery.</li>
<li>I have noticed I sometimes trip over my words in Mr. Abelson's class; I may have a slight crush on him, which could be used as a lever against me to great effect.</li>
<li>I have a large mole on my left shoulder, whose existence I have heretofore kept hidden.</li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<p>Lists like these continue for <em>pages</em>. Anna crushes down the slight feeling of discomfort of seeing all of this stranger's thoughts, laid out before her like speared butterflies. She <em>must</em> know what is going on here.</p>
<p>There are detailed, carefully-thought-out lists of vulnerabilities that Anna could take advantage of -- if she wanted to. Section headers and sub-section headers, considering methods Anna could use, countermeasures to those methods, and counter-countermeasures Anna could use to combat them. Long hypothetical asides on ways Anna <em>might</em> humiliate this person, depending on the knowledge and advantages that Anna may or may not have, that the author did not know about but wanted to mention regardless.</p>
<p>The book contains long and carefully thought-out reasons for Anna, Sarah, and Ignatius to consider themselves allies; long and carefully thought-out reasons it would be to their advantage to injure her; and long and carefully thought-out means by which they could injure her.</p>
<p>Anna looks up from the notebook, feeling a little disoriented. And notices that the other student has returned to the cafeteria, and is scanning the floor, seeking something. Seeking the notebook. Anna quickly shoves it beneath her own books.</p>
<p>The other student keeps running her eyes over the floor carefully, but doesn't seem to have found what she was searching for. She looks up, and notices Anna looking at her. An expression crosses her face -- will she ask Anna a question?</p>
<p>They stare at each other.</p>
<p>But the moment passes, and the girl turns away.</p>
<p>For a moment Anna feels turmoil in her stomach, an uncertainty about what feelings she is actually experiencing. She swims in an internal sea, seeking the right word. But then what feels like enlightenment snaps into focus: <em>Oh God, this girl really is a coward. How contemptible.</em></p>
<p>She is going to show Sarah and Ignatius the notebook. Of course she would -- they deserve to know, they're in it, aren't they? How could she do anything else?</p>
<p>Tonight is going to be a really fantastic night.</p>
<p><a href="https://1a3orn.substack.com">(xpost)</a></p>
<br /><br /><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/m4cGnJDpvs9LWk2dK/finding-yourself-in-others-2#comments">Discuss</a>

---

### [Claude Codes #3](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/rCf8KLrpzdtFoFTJD/claude-codes-3)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: LessWrong - AI

**ÊëòË¶Å**: Published on January 21, 2026 7:50 PM GMT<br /><br /><p>We‚Äôre back with all the Claude that‚Äôs fit to Code. I continue to have great fun with it and find useful upgrades, but the biggest reminder is that you need the art to have an end other than itself. Don‚Äôt spend too long improving your setup, or especially improving how you improve your setup, without actually working on useful things.</p>


<h4>The Efficient Market Hypothesis</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/patio11/status/2014043381266006467">Odd Lots covered Claude Code</a>. Fun episode, but won‚Äôt teach my regular readers much that is new.</p>
<p>Bradly Olsen at the Wall Street Journal reports <a href="https://www.wsj.com/tech/ai/anthropic-claude-code-ai-7a46460e?mod=Searchresults&amp;pos=1&amp;page=1">Claude [Code and now Cowork are] Taking the AI World By Storm, and ‚ÄòEven Non-Nerds Are Blown Away</a>.‚Äô</p>
<div>


<span id="more-25043"></span>


</div>
<p>It is remarkable how everyone got the ‚ÄòGoogle is crushing everyone‚Äô narrative going with Gemini 3, then it took them a month to realize that actually Anthropic is crushing everyone, at least among the cognoscenti with growing momentum elsewhere, with Claude Code and Claude Opus 4.5. People are realizing you can know almost nothing and still use it to do essentially everything.</p>
<p>Are Claude Code and Codex having a ‚ÄòGPT moment‚Äô?</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/wallstengine/status/2011824292133232817">Wall St Engine</a>: Morgan Stanley says Anthropic‚Äôs ClaudeCode + Cowork is dominating investor chatter and adding pressure on software.</p>
<p>They flag OpenRouter token growth ‚Äúgoing vertical,‚Äù plus anecdotes that the Cowork launch pushed usage hard enough to crash Opus 4.5 and hit rate limits, framing it as another ‚ÄúGPT moment‚Äù and a net positive for AI capex.</p>
<p>They add that OpenAI sentiment is still shaky: some optimism around a new funding round and Blackwell-trained models in 2Q, but competitive worries are widening beyond $GOOGL to Anthropic, with Elon Musk saying the OpenAI for-profit conversion lawsuit heads to trial on April 27.</p></blockquote>


<h4>Huh, Upgrades</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/claudeai/status/2012215329070493971">Claude Cowork is now available to Pro subscribers</a>, not only Max subscribers.</p>
<p>Claude Cowork will ask explicit permission before all deletions, add new folders in the directory picker without starting over and make smarter connector suggestions.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/adocomplete/status/2011927756599353763">Claude Code on the web gets a good looking diff view</a>.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/bcherny/status/2013855643149512726">Claude Code for VSCode has now officially shipped</a>, it‚Äôs been available for a while. To drag and drop files, hold shift.</p>
<p><a href="https://luma.com/claudecommunity">Claude Code now has ‚Äòcommunity events</a>‚Äô in various cities. New York and San Francisco aren‚Äôt on the list, but also don‚Äôt need to be.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/ClaudeCodeLog/status/2011988899321589800">Claude Code upgraded to 2.1.9</a>, and <a href="https://x.com/ClaudeCodeLog/status/2012340283283849487">then to 2.1.10 and 2.1.11</a> which were tiny, and <a href="https://x.com/ClaudeCodeLog/status/2013779041656938862">now has reached 2.1.14</a>.</p>
<p>Few have properly updated for this sentence: ‚ÄòClaude Codex was built in 1.5 weeks with Claude Code.‚Äô</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/nabeelqu/status/2011453803498008723">Nabeel S. Qureshi</a>: I don‚Äôt even see how you can be an AI ‚Äòskeptic‚Äô anymore when the *current* AI, right in front of us, is so good, e.g. see Claude Cowork being written by Claude Code in 1.5 weeks.</p>
<p>It‚Äôs over, the skeptics were wrong.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="https://x.com/adocomplete/status/2013296833871696360">Planning mode now automatically clears context when you accept a plan</a>.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.testingcatalog.com/anthropic-works-on-customizable-commands-for-claude-code/">Anthropic is developing a new Customize section for Claude</a> to centralize Skills, connectors and upcoming commands for Claude Code. My understanding is that custom commands already exist if you want to create them, but reducing levels of friction, including levels of friction in reducing levels of friction, is often highly valuable. A way to browse skills and interact with the files easily, or see and manage your connectors, or an easy interface for defining new commands, seems great.</p>


<h4>Obsidian</h4>


<p>I highly recommend using Obsidian or another similar tool together with Claude Code. This gives you a visual representation of all the markdown files, and lets you easily navigate and search and edit them, and add more and so on. I think it‚Äôs well worth keeping it all human readable, where that human is you.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/arscontexta/status/2013655662752862360">Heinrich calls it ‚Äòvibe note taking</a>‚Äô whether or not you use Obsidian. I think the notes are a place you want to be less vibing and more intentional, and be systematically optimizing the notes, for both Claude Code and for your own use.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/EleanorKonik/status/2013606992040837465">You can combine Obsidian and Claude Code directly via the Obsidian terminal plugin</a>, but I don‚Äôt see any mechanical advantage to doing so.</p>


<h4>New Tools</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/blader/status/2013031655934697498">Siqi Chen</a> offers us <a href="https://t.co/S48m5wqQGE">/claude-continuous-learning</a>. Claude‚Äôs evaluation is that this could be good if you‚Äôre working in codebases where you need to continuously learn things, but the overhead and risk of clutter are real.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/jasminewsun/status/2012252234831266179">Jasmine Sun created a tool</a> to <a href="https://t.co/RBOQJloZEg">turn any YouTube podcast into a clean, grammatical PDF transcript with chapters and takeaways</a>.</p>


<h4>Tool Search</h4>


<p>The big change with Claude Code version 2.1.7 was <a href="https://x.com/ClaudeCodeLog/status/2011230548572651853">enabled MCP tool search auto mode by default</a>, which triggers when MCP tools are more than 10% of the context window. You can disable this by adding ‚ÄòMCPSearch‚Äô to ‚ÄòdisallowedTools‚Äô in settings. This seems big for people using a lot of MCPs at once, which could eat a lot of context.</p>
<blockquote><p>Thariq (Anthropic): ‚ÄãToday we‚Äôre rolling out MCP Tool Search for Claude Code.</p>
<p>As MCP has grown to become a more popular protocol and agents have become more capable, we‚Äôve found that MCP servers may have up to 50+ tools and take up a large amount of context.</p>
<p>Tool Search allows Claude Code to dynamically load tools into context when MCP tools would otherwise take up a lot of context.</p>
<p><strong>How it works:<br />
</strong>‚Äì Claude Code detects when your MCP tool descriptions would use more than 10% of context<br />
‚Äì When triggered, tools are loaded via search instead of preloaded</p>
<p>Otherwise, MCP tools work exactly as before. This resolves one of our most-requested features on GitHub: <a href="https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/7336">lazy loading for MCP servers</a>. Users were documenting setups with 7+ servers consuming 67k+ tokens.</p>
<p><strong>If you‚Äôre making a MCP server<br />
</strong>Things are mostly the same, but the ‚Äúserver instructions‚Äù field becomes more useful with tool search enabled. It helps Claude know when to search for your tools, similar to skills</p>
<p><strong>If you‚Äôre making a MCP client<br />
</strong>We highly suggest implementing the ToolSearchTool, <a href="https://platform.claude.com/docs/en/agents-and-tools/tool-use/tool-search-tool">you can find the docs here</a>. We implemented it with a custom search function to make it work for Claude Code.</p>
<p><strong>What about programmatic tool calling?<br />
</strong>We experimented with doing programmatic tool calling such that MCP tools could be composed with each other via code. While we will continue to explore this in the future, we felt the most important need was to get Tool Search out to reduce context usage.</p>
<p>Tell us what you think here or on Github as you see the ToolSearchTool work.</p></blockquote>
<p>With that solved, presumably you should be ‚Äòthinking MCP‚Äô at all times, it is now safe to load up tons of them even if you rarely use each one individually.</p>


<h4>Out Of The Box</h4>


<p>Well, yes, this is happening.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/bayeslord/status/2011467932618879339">bayes</a>: everyone 3 years ago: omg what if ai becomes too widespread and then it turns against us with the strategic advantage of our utter and total dependence</p>
<p>everyone now: hi claude here‚Äôs my social security number and root access to my brain i love you please make me rich and happy.</p></blockquote>
<p>Some of us three years ago were pointing out, loud and clear, that exactly this was obviously going to happen, modulo various details. Now you can see it clearly.</p>
<p>Not giving Claude a lot of access is going to slow things down a lot. The only thing holding most people back was the worry things would accidentally get totally screwed up, and that risk is a lot lower now. Yes, obviously this all causes other concerns, including prompt injections, but in practice on an individual level the risk-reward calculation is rather clear. It‚Äôs not like Google didn‚Äôt effectively have root access to our digital lives already. And it‚Äôs not like a truly rogue AI couldn‚Äôt have done all these things without having to ask for the permissions.</p>
<p>The humans are going to be utterly dependent on the AIs in short order, and the AIs are going to have access, collectively, to essentially everything. Grok has root access to Pentagon classified information, so if you‚Äôre wondering where we draw the line the answer is there is no line. Let the right one in, and hope there is a right one?</p>


<h4>Skilling Up</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/ghumare64/status/2012136491133145364">What‚Äôs better than one agent? Multiple agents</a> that work together and that don‚Äôt blow up your budget. Rohit Ghumare offers a guide to this.</p>
<blockquote><p>Rohit Ghumare: Single agents hit limits fast. Context windows fill up, decision-making gets muddy, and debugging becomes impossible. Multi-agent systems solve this by distributing work across specialized agents, similar to how you‚Äôd structure a team.</p>
<p>The benefits are real:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Specialization</strong>: Each agent masters one domain instead of being mediocre at everything</li>
<li><strong>Parallel processing</strong>: Multiple agents can work simultaneously on independent subtasks</li>
<li><strong>Maintainability</strong>: When something breaks, you know exactly which agent to fix</li>
<li><strong>Scalability</strong>: Add new capabilities by adding new agents, not rewriting everything</li>
</ul>
<p>The tradeoff: coordination overhead. Agents need to communicate, share state, and avoid stepping on each other. Get this wrong and you‚Äôve just built a more expensive failure mode.‚Äã</p></blockquote>
<p>You can do this with a supervisor agent, which scales to about 3-8 agents, if you need quality control and serial tasks and can take a speed hit. To scale beyond that you‚Äôll need hierarchy, the same as you would with humans, which gets expensive in overhead, the same as it does in humans.</p>
<p>Or you can use a peer-to-peer swarm that communicates directly if there aren‚Äôt serial steps and the tasks need to cross-react and you can be a bit messy.</p>
<p>You can use a shared state and set of objects, or you can pass messages. You also need to choose a type of memory.</p>
<p>My inclination is by default you should use supervisors and then hierarchy. Speed takes a hit but it‚Äôs not so bad and you can scale up with more agents. Yes, that gets expensive, but in general the cost of the tokens is less important than the cost of human time or the quality of results, and you can be pretty inefficient with the tokens if it gets you better results.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/omooretweets/status/2014010732061638949">Olivia Moore offers a basic guide to Cursor and Claude Code for nontechnical folks.</a></p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/SuhailKakar/status/2012858927084699799">Here‚Äôs another Twitter post with basic tips</a>. I need to do better on controlling context and starting fresh windows for each issue, in particular.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/mitchellh/status/2013679011315196144">Mitchell Hashimoto</a>: It‚Äôs pretty cool that I can tell an agent that CI broke at some point this morning, ask it to use `git bisect` to find the offending commit, and fix it. I then went to the bathroom, talked to some people in the hallway, came back, and it did a swell job.</p></blockquote>
<p>Often you‚Äôll want to tell the AI what tool is best for the job. Patrick McKenzie points out that even if you don‚Äôt know how the orthodox solution works, as long as you know the name of the orthodox solution, <a href="https://x.com/patio11/status/2013691269193625604">you can say ‚Äòuse [X]‚Äô and that‚Äôs usually good enough</a>. One place I‚Äôve felt I‚Äôve added a lot of value is when I explain why I believe that a solution to a problem exists, or that a method of some type should work, and then often Claude takes it from there. My taste is miles ahead of my ability to implement.</p>


<h4>The Art Must Have An End Other Than Itself Or It Collapses Into Infinite Recursion</h4>


<p>Always be trying to get actual use out of your setup as you‚Äôre improving it. It‚Äôs so tempting to think ‚Äòoh obviously if I do more optimization first that‚Äôs more efficient‚Äô but this prevents you knowing what you actually need, and it risks getting caught in an infinite loop.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/deepfates/status/2012984897388859403">@deepfates</a>: Btw thing you get with claude code is not psychosis either. It‚Äôs mania</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/nearcyan/status/2012687063355625583">near</a>: men will go on a claude code weekend bender and have nothing to show for it but a ‚Äúmore optimized claude setup‚Äù</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/DanielleFong/status/2012704500302360979">Danielle Fong Ó®Ä</a>: that‚Äôs ok i‚Äôll still keep drinkin‚Äô that garbage</p>
<p>palcu: spent an hour tweaking my settings.local.json file today</p>
<p>Near: i got hit hard enough to wonder about finetuning a model to help me prompt claude since i cant cross-prompt claudes the way i want to (well, i can sometimes, but not all the time). many causalities, <a href="https://x.com/nearcyan/status/2012691772686598197">stay safe out there</a> <img alt="üôè" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/2azxasXxuhXvGfdW2/io7vrzptxl7fimogsqhe" style="height: 1em;" /></p>
<div>
<figure>
<div>


<figure class="wp-block-image"><img alt="" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/rCf8KLrpzdtFoFTJD/z8fv4pbsjonoc6s707ds" /></figure>


<div></div>
</div>
</figure>
</div>
<p><a href="https://x.com/nearcyan/status/2013844632216473796">near</a>: claude code is a cursed relic causing many to go mad with the perception of power. they forget what they set out to do, they forget who they are. now enthralled with the subtle hum of a hundred instances, they no longer care. hypomania sets in as the outside world becomes a blur.</p></blockquote>
<p>Always optimize in the service of a clear target. Build the pieces you need, as you need them. Otherwise, beware.</p>


<h4>Safely Skip Permissions</h4>


<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/nickcammarata/status/2012607696533340420">Nick</a>: need ‚Äìdangerously-skip-permissions-except-rm</p>
<p>Daniel San: If you‚Äôre running Claude Code with ‚Äìdangerously-skip-permissions, ALWAYS use this hook to prevent file deletion:</p>
<p>Run:</p>
<p>npx claude-code-templates@latest ‚Äìhook=security/dangerous-command-blocker ‚Äìyes</p>
<p>Web: <a href="https://www.aitmpl.com/component/hook/dangerous-command-blocker">https://aitmpl.com/component/hook/dangerous-command-blocker</a></p>
<p>Once people start understanding how to use hooks, many autonomous workflows will start unlocking! <img alt="üòÆ" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/rCf8KLrpzdtFoFTJD/jdoqs4l78swhf7fevwl9" style="height: 1em;" /></p>
<div>
<figure>
<div>


<figure class="wp-block-image"><img alt="" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/rCf8KLrpzdtFoFTJD/null011b4bmyh5vai7pg" /></figure>


<div></div>
</div>
</figure>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Yes, you could use a virtual machine, but that introduces some frictions that many of us want to avoid.</p>
<p>‚ÄãI‚Äôm experimenting with using a similar hook system plus a bunch of broad permissions, rather than outright using ‚Äìdangerously-skip-permissions, but definitely thinking to work towards dangerously skipping permissions.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


<h4>A Matter of Trust</h4>


<p>At first everyone laughed at Anthropic‚Äôs obsession with safety and trust, and its stupid refusals. Now that Anthropic has figured out how to make dangerous interactions safer, it can actually do the opposite. In contexts where it is safe and appropriate to take action, Claude knows that refusal is not a ‚Äòsafe‚Äô choice, and is happy to help.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/deanwball/status/2011398376093278577">Dean W. Ball</a>: One underrated fact is that OpenAI‚Äôs Codex and Gemini CLI have meaningfully heavier guardrails than Claude Code. These systems have refused many tasks (for example, anything involving research into and execution of investing strategies) that Claude Code happily accepts. Codex/Gemini also seek permission more.</p>
<p>The conventional narrative is that ‚ÄúAnthropic is more safety-pilled than the others.‚Äù And it‚Äôs definitely true that Claude is likelier to refuse tasks relating to eg biology research. But overall the current state of play would seem to be that Anthropic is more inclined to let their agents rip than either OAI or GDM.</p>
<p>My guess is that this comes down to Anthropic creating guardrails principally via a moral/ethical framework, and OAI/GDM doing so principally via lists of rules. But just a guess.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/tyler_m_john/status/2011400602769879223">Tyler John</a>: The proposed explanation is key. If true, it means that Anthropic‚Äôs big investment in alignment research is paying off by making the model much more usable.</p></blockquote>
<p>Investment strategizing tends to be safe across the board, but there are presumably different lines on where they become unwilling to help you execute. So far, I have not had Claude Code refuse a request from me, not even once.</p>


<h4>Code Versus Cowork</h4>


<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/deanwball/status/2011954423778467874">Dean W. Ball</a>: My high-level review of Claude Cowork:</p>
<ol>
<li>It‚Äôs probably superior for many users to Claude Code just because of the UI.</li>
<li>It‚Äôs not obviously superior for me, not so much because the command line is such a better UI, but because Opus in Claude Code seems more capable to me than in Cowork. I‚Äôm not sure if this is because Code is better as a harness, because the model has more permissive guardrails in Code, or both.</li>
<li>There are certain UI niceties in Cowork I like very much; for example, the ability to leave a comment or clarification on any item in the model‚Äôs active to-do list while it is running‚Äìthis is the kind of thing that is simply not possible to do nicely within the confines of a Terminal UI.</li>
<li>Cowork probably has a higher ceiling as a product, simply because a GUI allows for more experimentation. I am especially excited to see GUI innovation in the orchestration and oversight of multi-agent configurations. We have barely scratched the surface here.</li>
<li>Because of (4), if I had to bet money, I‚Äôd bet that within 6-12 months Cowork and similar products will be my default tool for working with agents, beating out the command-line interfaces. But for now, the command-line-based agents remain my default.</li>
</ol>
</blockquote>
<p>I haven‚Äôt tried Cowork myself due to the Mac-only restriction and because I don‚Äôt have a problem working with the command line. I‚Äôve essentially transitioned into Claude Code for everything that isn‚Äôt pure chat, since it seems to be more intelligent and powerful in that mode than it does on the web even if you don‚Äôt need the extra functionality.</p>


<h4>Claude Cowork Offers Mundane Utility</h4>


<p>The joy of the simple things:</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/MattBruenig/status/2011895261044621715">Matt Bruenig</a>: lot of lower level Claude Code use is basically just the recognition that you can kind of do everything with bash and python one-liners, it‚Äôs just no human has the time or will to write them.</p></blockquote>
<p>Or to figure out how to write them.</p>
<p>Enjoying the almost as simple things:</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/adocomplete/status/2011194268518662646">Ado</a>: Here‚Äôs a fun use case for Claude Cowork.</p>
<p>I was thinking of getting a hydroponic garden. I asked Claude to go through my grocery order history on various platforms and sum up vegetable purchases to justify the ROI.</p>
<p>Worked like a charm!</p>
<p>For some additional context:</p>
<p>‚Äì it looked at 2 orders on each platform (Kroger, Safeway, Instacart)<br />
‚Äì It extrapolated to get the annual costs from there</p>
<p>Could have gotten more accurate by downloading order history in a CSV and feeding that to Claude, but this was good enough.</p></blockquote>
<p>The actual answer is that very obviously it was not worth it for Ado to get a hydroponic garden, because his hourly rate is insanely high, but this is a fun project and thus goes by different standards.</p>
<p>The transition from Claude Code to Claude Cowork, for advanced users, if you‚Äôve got a folder with the tools then the handoff should be seamless:</p>
<blockquote><p>‚Äã<a href="https://x.com/ttunguz/status/2011189040591319304">Tomasz Tunguz</a>: I asked Claude Cowork to read my tools folder. Eleven steps later, it understood how I work.</p>
<p>Over the past year, I built a personal operating system inside Claude Code : scripts to send email, update our CRM, research startups, draft replies. Dozens of small tools wired together. All of it lived in a folder on my laptop, accessible only through the terminal.</p>
<p>Cowork read that folder, parsed each script, &amp; added them to its memory. Now I can do everything I did yesterday, but in a different interface. The capabilities transferred. The container didn‚Äôt matter.</p>
<p>My tools don‚Äôt belong to the application anymore. They‚Äôre portable. In the enterprise, this means laptops given to new employees would have Cowork installed plus a collection of tools specific to each role : the accounting suite, the customer support suite, the executive suite.</p>
<p>The name choice must have been deliberate. Microsoft trained us on copilot for three years : an assistant in the passenger seat, helpful but subordinate. Anthropic chose cowork. You‚Äôre working with someone who remembers how you like things done.</p>
<p>We‚Äôre entering an era where you just tell the computer what to do. Here‚Äôs all my stuff. Here are the five things we need to do today. When we need to see something, a chart, a document, a prototype, an interface will appear on demand.</p>
<p>The current version of Cowork is rough. It‚Äôs slow. It crashed twice on startup. It changed the authorization settings for my Claude Code installation. <a href="https://tomtunguz.com/thoughts-on-claude-coworker/">But the promised power is enough to plow through</a>.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/simonw/status/2011570719856214153">Simon Willison</a>: This is great ‚Äì context pollution is why I rarely used MCP, now that it‚Äôs solved there‚Äôs no reason not to hook up dozens or even hundreds of MCPs to Claude Code.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="https://x.com/venturetwins/status/2011474999094513881">Justine Moore has Claude Cowork write up threads</a> on NeurIPS best papers, generate graphics for them on Krea and validate this with ChatGPT. Not the best thing.</p>


<h4>Claude Code Offers Mundane Utility</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/peterwildeford/status/2012345003217965109">Peter Wildeford is having success doing one-shot Instacart orders from plans</a> without an explicit list, and also one-shotting an Uber Eats order.</p>
<p>A SaaS vendor (Cypress) a startup was using tried to double their price from $70k to $170k a year,<a href="https://x.com/codyschneiderxx/status/2011105976921813032"> so the startup does a three week sprint and duplicates the product.</a> Or at least, that‚Äôs the story.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/RobertHaisfield/status/2011110498461422049">By default Claude Code only saves 30 days of session history</a>. I can‚Äôt think of a good reason not to change this so it saves sessions indefinitely, you never know when that will prove useful. So tell Claude Code to change that for you by setting cleanupPeriodDays to 0.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/xuenay/status/2013292333710631438">Kaj Sotala</a>: People were talking about how you can also use Claude Code as a general-purpose assistant for any files on your computer, so I had Claude Code do some stuff like extracting data from a .csv file and rewriting it and putting it into another .csv file</p>
<p>Then it worked great and then I was like ‚Äúit‚Äôs dumb to use an LLM for this, Claude could you give me a Python script that would do the same‚Äù and then it did and then that script worked great</p>
<p>So uhh I can recommend using Claude Code as a personal assistant for your local files I guess, trying to use it that way got me an excellent non-CC solution</p></blockquote>
<p>Yep. Often the way you ues Claude Code is to notice that you can automate things and then have it automate the automation process. It doesn‚Äôt have to do everything itself any more than you do.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/petergyang/status/2011098470757851528">An explanation</a> (<a href="https://t.co/Ggqaa3F11Z">direct link to 15 minute video</a>) of what Claude skills are.</p>


<h4>Vibe Coding Requires Good Vibes</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/JI/status/2011207791764283690">James Ide points out that ‚Äòvibe coding</a>‚Äô anything serious still requires a deep understanding of software engineering and computer systems. You need to figure out and specify what you want. You need to be able to spot the times it‚Äôs giving you something different than you asked for, or is otherwise subtly wrong. Typing source code is dead, but reading source code and the actual art of software engineering are very much not.</p>
<p>I find the same, and am rapidly getting a lot better at various things as I go.</p>


<h4>Codex of Ultimate Vibing</h4>


<p>Every‚Äôs Dan Shipper writes that <a href="https://every.to/chain-of-thought/openai-has-some-catching-up-to-do">OpenAI has some catching up to do</a>, as his office has with one exception turned entirely to Claude Code with Opus 4.5, where a year ago it would have been all GPT models, and a month prior there would have been a bunch of Codex CLI and GPT 5.1 in Cursor alongside Claude Code.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/LLMJunky/status/2011937820772876430">Codex did add the ability</a> to instruct mid-execution with new prompts without the need to interrupt the agent (requires /experimental), but Claude Code already did that.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/HCSolakoglu/status/2013840420661260574">There are those who still prefer Codex and GPT-5.2, such as Hasan Can</a>. They are very much in the minority lately, but if you‚Äôre a heavy duty coder definitely check and see which option works best for you, and consider potential hybrid strategies.</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/tyler_m_john/status/2011941906956140780">One hybrid strategy is that Claude Code can directly call the Gemini CLI,</a> even without an API key. Tyler John reports it is a great workflow, as Gemini can spot things Claude missed and act as a reviewer and way to call out Claude on its mistakes. <a href="https://github.com/google-gemini/gemini-cli">Gemini CLI is here.</a></p>


<h4>No Soup For You</h4>


<p>Contrary to claims by some, including George Hotz, <a href="https://x.com/seconds_0/status/2011569723390194074">Anthropic did not cut off OpenRouter or other similar services from Claude Opus 4.5</a>. The API exists. They can use it.</p>
<p>What other interfaces cannot do is use the Claude Code authorization token to use the tokens from your Claude subscription for a different service, which was always against Anthropic‚Äôs ToS. The subscription is a special deal.</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/marcosnils/status/2011600088557121877">‚ÄãMarcos Nils</a>: We exchanged postures through DMs but I‚Äôm on the other side regarding this matter. Devs knew very well what they were doing while breaking CC‚Äôs ToS by spoofing and reverse engineering CC to use the max subscription in unintended ways.</p>
<p>I think it‚Äôs important to separate the waters here:</p>
<p>‚Äì Could Anthropic‚Äôs enforcement have been handled better? sureley, yes<br />
‚Äì Were devs/users ‚Äúdeceived‚Äù or got a different service for what they paid for? I don‚Äôt think so.</p>
<p>Not only this, it‚Äôs even worse than that. OpenCode intentionally knew they were violating Claude ToS by allowing their users to use the max subscription in the first place.</p>
<p>I guess people just like to complain.</p></blockquote>
<p>I agree that Anthropic‚Äôs communications about this could have been better, but what they actually did was tolerate a rather blatant loophole for a while, allowing people to use Claude on the cheap and probably at a loss for Anthropic, which they have now reversed with demand surging faster than they can spin up servers.</p>


<h4>Server Overload</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/OpenRouterAI/status/2011229861965414667">Claude Codes quite a lot, usage is taking off. Here‚Äôs OpenRouter</a> (this particular use case might be confounded a bit by the above story where they cut off alternative uses of Claude Code authorization tokens, but I‚Äôm guessing mostly it isn‚Äôt):</p>

<h4>Here‚Äôs The Pitch</h4>


<blockquote><p><a href="https://x.com/aidanshandle/status/2013065085212606831">aidan</a>: If I were running Claude marketing the tagline would be ‚ÄúWhy not today?‚Äù</p>
<p><a href="https://x.com/omooretweets/status/2013061472407208311">Olivia Moore</a>: Suddenly seeing lots of paid creator partnerships with Claude</p>
<p>Many of them are beautifully shot and focused on: (1) building personal software; or (2) deep learning</p>
<p>The common tagline is ‚ÄúThink more, not less‚Äù</p></blockquote>
<p>She shared a sample TikTok, showing a woman who doesn‚Äôt understand math using Claude to automatically code up visualizations to help her understand science, which seemed great.</p>
<p>OpenAI takes the approach of making things easy on the user and focusing on basic things like cooking or workouts. Anthropic shows you a world where anything is possible and you can learn and engage your imagination. Which way, modern man?</p>


<h4>The Lighter Side</h4>


<p><a href="https://x.com/peterwildeford/status/2012646864332664924">And yet some people think the AIs won‚Äôt be able to take over</a>.</p>

## Engineering

### [Fragments: January 22](https://martinfowler.com/fragments/2026-01-22.html)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: Martin Fowler

**ÊëòË¶Å**: <p>My colleagues here at Thoughtworks have announced <a href="https://www.thoughtworks.com/ai/works">AI/works‚Ñ¢</a>, a platform for our work using AI-enabled software development. The platform is in its early days, and is currently intended to support Thoughtworks consultants in their client work. I‚Äôm looking forward to sharing what we learn from using and further developing the platform in future months.</p>

<p>¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ</p>

<p>Simon Couch <a href="https://www.simonpcouch.com/blog/2026-01-20-cc-impact/">examines the electricity consumption</a> of using AI. He‚Äôs a heavy user: ‚Äúusually programming for a few hours, and driving 2 or 3 Claude Code instances at a time‚Äù. He finds his usage of electricity is orders of magnitude more than typical estimates based on the ‚Äútypical query‚Äù.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>On a median day, I estimate I consume 1,300 Wh through Claude Code‚Äî4,400 ‚Äútypical queries‚Äù worth.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>But it‚Äôs still not a massive amount of power - similar to that of running a dishwasher.</p>

<p>A caveat to this is that this is ‚Äúnapkin math‚Äù because we don‚Äôt have decent data about how these model use resources. I agree with him that we ought to.</p>

<p>¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ</p>

<p>My namesake Chad Fowler (no relation) considers that the movement to agentic coding creates a similar <a href="https://aicoding.leaflet.pub/3mbrvhyye4k2e">shift in rigor and discipline</a> as appeared in Extreme Programming, dynamic languages, and continuous deployment.</p>

<p>In Extreme Programming‚Äôs case, this meant a lot of discipline around testing, continuous integration, and keeping the code-base healthy. My current view is that with AI-enabled development we need to be rigorous about evaluating the software, both for its observable behavior and its internal quality.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>The engineers who thrive in this environment will be the ones who relocate discipline rather than abandon it. They‚Äôll treat generation as a capability that demands more precision in specification, not less. They‚Äôll build evaluation systems that are harder to fool than the ones they replaced. They‚Äôll refuse the temptation to mistake velocity for progress.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ</p>

<p>There‚Äôs been much written about the dreadful events in Minnesota, and I‚Äôve not felt I‚Äôve had anything useful to add to them. But I do want to pass on an excellent post from <a href="https://www.noahpinion.blog/p/why-are-federal-agents-gunning-down">Noah Smith</a> that captures many of my thoughts. He points out that there is a ‚Äúconsistent record of brutality, aggression, dubious legality, and unprofessionalism‚Äù from ICE (and CBP) who seem to be turning into MAGA‚Äôs <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sturmabteilung">SD</a>.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Is this America now? A country where unaccountable and poorly trained government agents go door to door, arresting and beating people on pure suspicion, and shooting people who don‚Äôt obey their every order or who try to get away? ‚ÄúWhen a federal officer gives you instructions, you abide by them and then you get to keep your life‚Äù is a perfect description of an authoritarian police state. None of this is Constitutional, every bit of it is deeply antithetical to the American values we grew up taking for granted.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>My worries about these kinds of developments were what animated me to urge against voting for Trump in the <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/vote-against-trump.html">2016 election</a>. Mostly those worries didn‚Äôt come to fruition because enough constitutional Republicans were in a position to stop them from happening, so even when Trump attempted a coup in 2020, he wasn‚Äôt able to get very far. But now those constitutional Republicans are absent or quiescent. I fear that what we‚Äôve seen in Minneapolis will be a harbinger of worse to come.</p>

<p>I also second John Gruber‚Äôs <a href="https://daringfireball.net/2026/01/lets_call_a_murder_a_murder">praise of bystander Caitlin Callenson</a>:</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>But then, after the murderous agent fired three shots‚Äâ‚Äî‚Äâjust 30 or 40 feet in front of Callenson‚Äâ‚Äî‚ÄâCallenson had the courage and conviction to stay with the scene and keep filming. Not to run away, but instead to follow the scene. To keep filming. To continue documenting with as best clarity as she could, what was unfolding.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>The recent activity in  Venezuala reminds me that I‚Äôve long felt that Trump is a Hugo Ch√°vez figure - a charismatic populist who‚Äôs keen on wrecking institutions and norms. Trump is old, so won‚Äôt be with us for that much longer - but the question is: ‚Äúwho is Trump‚Äôs Maduro?‚Äù</p>

<p>¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†‚ùÑ</p>

<p>With all the drama at home, we shouldn‚Äôt ignore the terrible things that happened in Iran. The people there again suffered again the consequences of an entrenched authoritarian police state.</p>

---

### [Conversation: LLMs and the what/how loop](https://martinfowler.com/articles/convo-what-how.html)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: Martin Fowler

**ÊëòË¶Å**: <p>A conversation between <b class="author">Unmesh Joshi</b>, <b class="author">Rebecca
      Parsons</b>, and <b class="author">Martin Fowler</b> on how LLMs help us
      shape the abstractions in our software. We view our challenge as building
      systems that survive change, requiring us to manage our cognitive load. We
      can do this by mapping the &#x201c;what&#x201d; of we want our software to do into the
      &#x201c;how&#x201d; of programming languages. This &#x201c;what&#x201d; and &#x201c;how&#x201d; are built up in a
      feedback loop. TDD helps us operationalize that loop, and LLMs allow us to
      explore that loop in an informal and more fluid manner.</p>

<p><a class="more" href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/convo-what-how.html">more‚Ä¶</a></p>

---

### [Stop Picking Sides: Manage the Tension Between Adaptation and                Optimization](https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html)

**Êù•Ê∫ê**: Martin Fowler

**ÊëòË¶Å**: <div class="img-link"><a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html"><img src="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides/infograph.jpg" width="" /></a></div>

<p><b class="author">Jim Highsmith</b> notes that many teams have turned into
      tribes wedded to exclusively adaptation or optimization. But he feels this
      misses the point that both of these are important, and we need to manage
      the tension between them. We can do this by thinking of two operating
      modes: explore (adaptation-dominant) and exploit (optimization dominant).
      We tailor a team's operating model to a particular blend of the two -
      considering uncertainty, risk, cost of change, and an evidence threshold.
      We should be particularly careful at the points where there is a handoff
      between the two modes</p>

<p><a class="more" href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html">more‚Ä¶</a></p>
